      <div id="content-column">
       <div class="narrowcolumn" id="content">
        <div class="post-1796 comic type-comic status-publish has-post-thumbnail hentry chapters-comic uentry postonpage-1 odd post-author-gavin" id="post-1796">
         <div class="post-content">
          <div class="post-info">
           <h2 class="post-title">
            109. ZEN FABLE: The river crossing
           </h2>
           <div class="post-text">
            <span class="post-author">
             by
             <a href="http://zenpencils.com/author/gavin/" rel="author">
              Gav
             </a>
            </span>
            <span class="posted-on">
             on
            </span>
            <span class="post-date">
             March 26, 2013
            </span>
            <div class="comic-post-info">
             <div class="comic-chapter">
              Chapter:
              <a href="http://zenpencils.com/chapter/comic/" rel="tag">
               Comic
              </a>
             </div>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="clear">
          </div>
          <div class="entry">
           <!-- AddThis Button BEGIN -->
           <div addthis:title="109. ZEN FABLE: The river crossing " addthis:url="http://zenpencils.com/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/" class="addthis_toolbox addthis_default_style ">
            <a class="addthis_button_facebook_like" fb:like:layout="button_count">
            </a>
            <a class="addthis_button_tweet">
            </a>
            <a class="addthis_button_google_plusone" g:plusone:size="medium">
            </a>
            <a class="addthis_button_pinterest_pinit">
            </a>
            <a class="addthis_counter addthis_pill_style">
            </a>
           </div>
           <!-- AddThis Button END -->
           <p>
            This is a well-known Buddhist story that has appeared in many different versions over the past century. There are even
            <a href="http://spiritsinharmony.blogspot.com.au/2008/02/two-monks-carry-woman.html">
             Catholic and Jewish
            </a>
            retellings. After some digging (with help from reader, Wayne P) the original author of the fable seems to be a famous Japanese Zen master named
            <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanzan">
             Tanzan
            </a>
            , who was the professor of philosophy during the Meiji period. This particular version of the story is taken from the great book
            <em>
             Zen in the Martial Arts
            </em>
            , by Joe Hyams, who was a student of Bruce Lee.
           </p>
           <p>
            I think the lesson of the story is pretty simple. The younger monk has become so trapped by dogma and rules that he has forgotten the most important rule, which is to help those in need. How did you interpret it?
           </p>
           <p class="notes">
            RELATED COMICS:
            <a href="http://zenpencils.com/comic/35-bruce-lee-absorb-what-is-useful/">
             Absorb what is useful.
            </a>
            <a href="http://zenpencils.com/comic/82-zen-proverb-the-master/">
             The Master.
            </a>
            <a href="http://zenpencils.com/comic/94-the-two-wolves/">
             The Two Wolves.
            </a>
           </p>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>

                <div class="comment-text">
                 <p>
                  Its not about breaking rules, but being honest about them, like Cicero once said. If youre looking for justification for your actions in the rules, youre not being hones about them, deeming the rule (or law, as he would have said) useless. Rules should aim towards justice, if theyre used for unfair purposes, they are not rules.
                 </p>

                <li class="comment even depth-4 ucomment" id="comment-25777">
                 <div class="comment-avatar">
                  <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/eec6076a32e0a24c705abde1e1537ed0?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/eec6076a32e0a24c705abde1e1537ed0?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="Halceon" width="64"/>
                 </div>
                 <div class="comment-content">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <cite>
                    Halceon
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="comment-meta-data">
                   <span class="comment-time" title="Friday, April 5th 2013, 1:21am">
                    April 5, 2013, 1:21 am
                   </span>
                   <span class="comment-permalink">
                    <span class="separator">
                     |
                    </span>
                    <a href="#comment-25777" title="Permalink to comment">
                     #
                    </a>
                   </span>
                   <span class="comment-reply-link">
                    <span class="separator">
                     |
                    </span>
                    <a aria-label="Reply to Halceon" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25777#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25777", "25777", "respond", "1796" )'>
                     Reply
                    </a>
                   </span>
                  </div>
                  <div class="comment-text">
                   <p>
                    Im going to side with Ginkgink here, but for different reasons. The specific language has very little to do with it. I believe the whole fable is about the nature of law.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    In a sense this fable illustrates the letter/spirit of the law distinction, but it takes it slightly further. I interpret this as the acolyte adhering to the letter of the law (no physical contact with women) and believing that to be the extent of the laws existence. On the other hand, the priest not only grasps the spirit of the law (no intimacy with women), but the goal of the law (no attachment).
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Therefore the priest can act counter to the letter, but in line with the spirit and goal of the law by carrying the woman and then letting her go off his back and off his mind. Meanwhile the acolyte, by focusing too much on the letter of the law has actually formed an attachment to the woman, even if it isnt intimate. In a sense, hes still carrying her.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    I believe this is a fable about understanding as a virtue, not self control as a virtue.
                   </p>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                 <div class="clear">
                 </div>

            <li class="comment even depth-2" id="comment-32791">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a458c4d941f0a6f5b01d924c1c3fc004?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a458c4d941f0a6f5b01d924c1c3fc004?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="bojo" width="64"/>
             </div>
             <div class="comment-content">
              <div class="comment-author vcard">
               <cite>
                bojo
               </cite>
              </div>
              <div class="comment-meta-data">
               <span class="comment-time" title="Thursday, August 29th 2013, 1:40am">
                August 29, 2013, 1:40 am
               </span>
               <span class="comment-permalink">
                <span class="separator">
                 |
                </span>
                <a href="#comment-32791" title="Permalink to comment">
                 #
                </a>
               </span>
               <span class="comment-reply-link">
                <span class="separator">
                 |
                </span>
                <a aria-label="Reply to bojo" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=32791#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-32791", "32791", "respond", "1796" )'>
                 Reply
                </a>
               </span>
              </div>
              <div class="comment-text">
               <p>
                Im going to have to disagree with your interpretation here.  The point of this fable has nothing to do with desire, but instead is a story about attachment.
               </p>
               <p>
                When the old monk says, I put the girl down on the other side of the stream, it means that his attachment to the girl and the breaking of rules for the sake of being a kind human being are things of the past, things not worthy of burdening the mind with any longer.
               </p>
               <p>
                His followup, Why are you still carrying her? implies that even though she is no longer relevant to the moment, the young monk is still burdening his mind with his attachment to her (not in the desire sense, but the object of focus sense), and her momentary relation to the old monk breaking the sects rules.
               </p>
               <p>
                One of Zen Buddhisms primary teachings is that attachment to not only material things, but attachment to ideas and notions of little or no consequence, all leads to suffering.  This is expressed in the story by the young monk fuming while he crosses the river, which could be interpreted as a form of suffering.
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>
             <div class="clear">
             </div>

                <div class="comment-text">
                 <p>
                  BRAVO, Bojo! Having studied Buddhism on my own time, at my own direction, I came to the same conclusion. Attachment, in its myriad forms, can be distracting and destructive  thats why letting go tends to feel so good.
                 </p>
                </div>
               </div>

            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              I think this is less about being trapped in a dogma, but more about being trapped in the past. So the message is more like: Dont dwell in the past! Carry on!
              <img alt=":-)" class="wp-smiley" src="http://cdn.zenpencils.com/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;"/>
             </p>
             <p>
              In a bigger context this would also mean not to carry the heavy burden of dogmatic rules from the past, but to live more presently and to decide your morals from moment to moment.
             </p>
            </div>

               <p>
                Additionally, I was also thinking that personal morals should be like the water in the river, always moving, flexible, adaptable. Bruce Lee already formulated it perfectly:
               </p>
               <p>
                Empty your mind, be formless, shapeless  like water. Now you put water in a cup, it becomes the cup; You put water into a bottle it becomes the bottle; You put it in a teapot it becomes the teapot. Now water can flow or it can crash. Be water, my friend.
               </p>
               <p>

            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              I interpret it on another way. The teacher says the novice is still carrying the woman because he is still thinking about her, when he should be focusing in the present, not in the past.
             </p>
             <p>
              As I undertand it, Zen is a lot into this: the only important thing is the present. If you fail at being aware of it, though, you must not worry and blame yourself, for your failure belongs to past.
             </p>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="comment-25042">
           <div class="comment-avatar">
            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a67854c33b38525ae793e6fc715effb2?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a67854c33b38525ae793e6fc715effb2?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="RoxyRocketeer" width="64"/>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-content">
            <div class="comment-author vcard">
             <cite>
              RoxyRocketeer
             </cite>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Tuesday, March 26th 2013, 8:48am">
              March 26, 2013, 8:48 am
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a href="#comment-25042" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to RoxyRocketeer" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25042#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25042", "25042", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              The version Ive heard doesnt have this particular dogma in it, so its likely a newer version. In this version, it is a woman with lots of packages from shopping and an elderly servant who is incapable of carrying her. She demands the monks carry her across, and when the older one does, she leaves without thanking him, but at least the servant no longer has to deal with her raving. The younger monk fumes about her rudeness, and when he finally says something to the older monk, the elder says the same line about why the younger was still carrying her. So in this instance it wasnt about dogma, but about the everyday annoyances that can cause great damage when a person holds onto anger, and about how a moment of sacrifice can improve another persons circumstances (the harried servant) even if it benefits someone undeserving. I think its also about letting go of the need for satisfaction, judgement and gratitude. Service should be service regardless of reward.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>

            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              Theres a version of this in the childrens book Zen Shorts by Jon Muth. Its the version where the woman is rude and has many packages and demands to be carried across the water. The younger monk fumes about it all day, and when he finally says something to the elder, thats when he asks why he is still carrying her.  Ive always loved this and I often think I should practice it more
             </p>
             <p>
              rick

             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Luis" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25045#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25045", "25045", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              I agree with Carles.
             </p>
             <p>
              The point of buddhsim is freeing your mind from falsehood, attachment and desire. I believe this is not so much about the monk being trapped in dogma, but being trapped in his own mind.
             </p>
             <p>
              For the elder, the act of carrying the girl across the river was a simple, untrascendental act, and thus his mind and spirit are not affected by it. The young one, on the other hand, cannot overcome the burden of his own thoughts, and he becomes enraged and loses his inner peace.
             </p>
             <p>
              The more inner peace one has, the more of that energy is transmitted to others. Thats how I see it.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>

               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              Gavin, I love your beautiful work!
              <br/>
              This story can be seen as a commentary on the purpose of Buddhism itself.  Both major schools, Mahayana and Hinayana (large and small Vehicles) are just that  rafts across the river from ignorance to enlightenment.  Once you reach the farther shore, you leave the raft behind.  Once you are Buddha, you leave Buddhism behind.  How wonderful!
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>

               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              I also agree with Carles; the acolyte is still focussed on the rules, not the meaning behind them.
             </p>
             <p>
              Poor old fall-guy acolytes always cop the rough end of the pineapple in these tales (something about the victors getting to write them?;-) The thing is, hed still be doing the hard yakka of learning the rules; laying down the muscle memory. (How many hours does it take to be an expert at anything? 10000?) His master has gone beyond that, and just does.
             </p>
             <p>
              I wonder if there are any Islamic or Hindu versions of this?
             </p>

             <span class="comment-time" title="Tuesday, March 26th 2013, 12:21pm">

             <span class="comment-permalink">

              <a href="#comment-25062" title="Permalink to comment">

             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Kaylee" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25062#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25062", "25062", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              When I first read that story, the lady was not nearly so kind,
              <br/>
              When the monks got to the side of the river, they saw a grand sedan chair, with a beautiful woman inside. It was so grand and decorated that the men could not carry it, and the lady, across the river.
              <br/>
              The older monk offered to carry her across.
              <br/>
              When they got to the other side, the lady jumped off his back, got in her chair and left, without thanking the kind monk.
              <br/>
              The rest of the story is very similar, with the younger monk fuming at the womans rudeness.
              <br/>
              Oh, and for some reason they were all animals (it was a childrens book)
             </p>
             <p>
              The lesson I always get from this story is to forgive and forget those who are rude or mean to you, and not to carry that negativity with you.
              <br/>
              But brilliant comic, ever time I open this website, they just get better and better.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>

          <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="comment-25076">
           <div class="comment-avatar">
            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2c15db92f4ce849e3cf37745ce5d6554?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2c15db92f4ce849e3cf37745ce5d6554?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="Mori" width="64"/>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-content">
            <div class="comment-author vcard">
             <cite>
              Mori
             </cite>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Tuesday, March 26th 2013, 3:11pm">
              March 26, 2013, 3:11 pm
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a href="#comment-25076" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Mori" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25076#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25076", "25076", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              The first reply says it well: it is about the difference between blind adherence and genuine wisdom.
             </p>
             <p>
              This is not so uncommon in Zen, as a general observation. A lot of zen tales and fables intentionally undercut the seriousness of zen studios, to try and get students to see that if they fixate too much on the rules and forms of zen, they will fail to understand zen.
             </p>
             <p>
              For example, another classic: a master sends a student to his new room and asks him to meditate. The student tries, but his room is in disrepair. The candles have worn down. Theres a leak in the roof. The floor is bare and cold. The student tries his best for days, but goes to the master in frustration. He says he cannot overcome the distractions in his room. His master goes to inspect the room. Then, the master asks him why he has not informed the groundskeeper that his room needs new candles, a rug, and a leak plugged.
             </p>
             <p>
              The student obviously was so focused on achieving the power of zen that he assumed every little practical distraction was something to tune out with willpower alone a pointless exercise when it was more productive to make his quarters comfortable so he could meditate in peace.
             </p>
             <p>
              Maybe all this is a reason why some Zen masters say if you meet someone who claims they are a Zen master, they arent.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>

          <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 microid-mailto+http:sha1:8817d007a4e2dca2c0a79a0ca516f610b98bdae4" id="comment-25091">
           <div class="comment-avatar">
            <a href="http://Www.outsidecontext.com" rel="external nofollow" title="Basho">
             <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/966313b3255f67a7395cfe91613cfc4b?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/966313b3255f67a7395cfe91613cfc4b?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="Basho" width="64"/>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-content">
            <div class="comment-author vcard">
             <cite title="http://Www.outsidecontext.com">
              <a class="external nofollow" href="http://Www.outsidecontext.com" title="Basho">
               Basho
              </a>
             </cite>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Tuesday, March 26th 2013, 5:55pm">
              March 26, 2013, 5:55 pm
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a href="#comment-25091" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Basho" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25091#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25091", "25091", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              This story is based on a passage in the Diamond Sutra:
             </p>
             <p>
              6.  Subhuti asked,  Lord, will there always be people who understand your message?
             </p>
             <p>
              Buddha answered,  Dont doubt it, Subhuti!  There will always be people who, hearing the message, will adhere to the precepts and practice our way.  Our message will reach people simply because it is true!  There will come a time when many will no longer need words, but will be beyond words.  We must all strive to go beyond the words, because words can be clung to, and we should not cling to things.  Understand that the words of the Buddha are like a raft built to cross a river:  When its purpose is completed, it must be left behind if we are to travel further!
             </p>
             <p>
              A value I personally hold dear. Thanks for the rendering
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="comment-25117">
           <div class="comment-avatar">
            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/342780dbc208bfd72394d836d19f0213?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/342780dbc208bfd72394d836d19f0213?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="Beth" width="64"/>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-content">
            <div class="comment-author vcard">
             <cite>
              Beth
             </cite>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Wednesday, March 27th 2013, 12:09am">
              March 27, 2013, 12:09 am
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a href="#comment-25117" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Beth" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25117#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25117", "25117", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              Its a rich message, but heres my favorite point to it:  the older monk has overcome his fear  of the woman, of sexuality, of the forbidden, of his own physical response.  He is not afraid of the possibility of mishap, and accordingly, is able to do a good deed, and help another person, without being blocked by his own fear.
             </p>
             <p>
              The younger man, trying to keep himself safe by vehemently rejecting his connection with a person who embodies his fear, has instead condemned himself to a prison of fear.  Not only that, its made him act like a jerk.  His fear has turned to anger and condemnation, to justify his refusal to help.  Hes taken the prohibition and made into a permanent, souring burden.
             </p>
             <p>
              This story could have made the same point in any number of ways, but it specifically chooses a young woman as the source of fear.  Thats not a random choice.  Its what makes this simple story so poweerful, right down to the present.  Misogyny, and other forms of bigotry, are an even greater problem today than they were in this era.  What we fear, we reject, and teach ourselves to hate.  We become strident and angry.  We denounce others who do not share our fear, people who act in tolerance and express kindness and compassion.  We demand that all others share out anger and hatred, or else become an object of hatred in turn.  And all this, we attempt to justify on the grounds of religious teachings and dogma  teachings that came from a source that also valued love, kindness, and charity.
             </p>
             <p>
              Sound familiar?
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 ucomment" id="comment-25127">
           <div class="comment-avatar">
            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/c2d4290b03d1a4f6f733e57632368430?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/c2d4290b03d1a4f6f733e57632368430?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="AlphaAnd" width="64"/>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-content">
            <div class="comment-author vcard">
             <cite>
              AlphaAnd
             </cite>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Wednesday, March 27th 2013, 1:17am">
              March 27, 2013, 1:17 am
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a href="#comment-25127" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to AlphaAnd" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25127#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25127", "25127", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              The meaning is: let it go, that is fundamental in Buddhism. The young acolyte portraits the casual listener, that would think at first that the action of the elder is contradictory. But it isnt, because the essence of the problem arent the women for the male monks: the cause of dukka (suffering, stress) is clinging, is the desire. Women are OK, what it isnt is Greed, that is one of the Poison.
             </p>
             <p>
              So the elder let his desire go, he doesnt depend on it. He his freed from the desire and he is free to help the girl: compassion is another fundamental concept, and there is this dilemma too, you are renouncing to compassion, but why? It is because of the *fear* of the desire and/or because you believe blindly in a rule  but if you read the Kalama Sutta, you understand that you shouldnt believe in something, just because your teacher told it. You should understand it better and deeper.
             </p>
             <p>
              Ideally, you dont even have to make an effort to avoid the girl  or better the desire  because here it comes dukka again. You have to let it go away, not to combat it and filling your mind with thoughts about how to combat it. Once you let go the thought and the desire, you are freed: you dont have to think to avoid it, it just have no influence on you. You are free.
             </p>
             <p>
              So once the elder left the girl, he no longer think of her (unlike the younger who will not sleep that night, thinking of the soft body of girl pressed on his back).
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1 ucomment" id="comment-25183">
           <div class="comment-avatar">
            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/294de3557d9d00b3d2d8a1e6aab028cf?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/294de3557d9d00b3d2d8a1e6aab028cf?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="Anonymous" width="64"/>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-content">
            <div class="comment-author vcard">
             <cite>
              Anonymous
             </cite>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Wednesday, March 27th 2013, 2:08pm">
              March 27, 2013, 2:08 pm
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a href="#comment-25183" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to " class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25183#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25183", "25183", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              Theres an Indian version of the story; where an old guru is being escorted by a young spiritual seeker. A bathing woman in the river is heard screaming for help. They find that shes fallen.
             </p>
             <p>
              The younger man is the rescuer in this version. He carries the naked woman out of the water and sets her down. Later on he gets heat from the older guru. He responds with the exact same punchline.
             </p>
             <p>
              The idea being that the older man is still trapped by dogma whereas the younger man has gone beyond.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">

             <p>
              i read a similar version, except towards the end, after they depart with the lady, they continue their path. few hours later, the young monk ask, confused, Why did you carry that lady when we are forbid to touch a women?
              <br/>
              old monk: Ive already left her behind miles away. why are you still carrying her?
              <br/>
              Interpretation: you still can touch/help a women, it is your state of mind that is important.
             </p>

            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Friday, March 29th 2013, 12:40am">
              March 29, 2013, 12:40 am

             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">

              <a href="#comment-25327" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Stuart" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25327#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25327", "25327", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              The older monk has picked up the forbidden woman, then let her go.
             </p>
             <p>
              The young monk, however, has fastened on to the woman what he thinks she represents (a rule has been broken), and he is so unable to let go of this that he explodes in anger.
             </p>
             <p>
              The young monk is carrying the burden of worrying about the rules around with him, which serves no purpose  he is harming himself.
             </p>
             <p>
              The rule is intended to avoid harm being caused, and here he is causing himself harm in the name of the rule!
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>

             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Davingsbury" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=25414#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-25414", "25414", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              You know it is rather funny to see people debating the best interpretation of the point of a zen fable considering how much of zen focuses on purpose in the moment and perception.
             </p>
             <p>
              The most valuable thing I learned from my scant studies of the subject was the realization that a chair is really only a chair when it is chairing. Outside of that its a heap of wood shaped into a certain form.  Another way to think of it is that the instant a bar-fight breaks out and someone cracks it over anther guys head its no longer really chairing but bludgeoning, therefore its now a weapon as far as its purpose goes.
             </p>
             <p>
              Hopefully that serves the point Id like to make that the point of any proverb or fable is what you interpret it and put it to use as.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>

            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              I interpret as below:
             </p>
             <p>
              The old monk was very well detached from the worldly pleasures; so carrying that woman, the young sibling, or some stone would have been same to him.
              <br/>
              That is why he could just drop of the lady(from his shoulder and mind) on the other side of the stream easily.
             </p>
             <p>
              And more importantly, I guess the monks life-style/rules prohibits worldly material pleasures, it doesnt prohibits them from touching any particular thing.
             </p>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="comment-31876">
           <div class="comment-avatar">
            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/ef61169c56197403e6571bbcb07dbb67?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/ef61169c56197403e6571bbcb07dbb67?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="Tug Brice" width="64"/>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-content">
            <div class="comment-author vcard">
             <cite>
              Tug Brice
             </cite>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Thursday, August 1st 2013, 11:52am">
              August 1, 2013, 11:52 am
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a href="#comment-31876" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Tug Brice" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=31876#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-31876", "31876", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              Had to chime in here because Zen in the Martial Arts is a truly amazing book (and the contents do justify the word).  I found it before I became a Buddhist and its still one of the most amazing volumes of practical Buddhist wisdom Ive ever found.
             </p>
             <p>
              Since Im here, I might as well drop my two cents in.  Since Ive read the book and thus heard this before, Ive had a lot of time to think about it.  Like most koans, there are many lessons here.  To me, the main one is about clear sight.
             </p>
             <p>
              The older monk saw things very differently than the young monk did.  What the older monk saw that the younger monk didnt was a chance to create happiness and joy.  Thats something I didnt see mentioned before.    By carrying the woman across the river, he spread joy into the world.  Lets assume for the moment that both of them were young monks and both declined.  The young woman would either still be waiting or would ruin her attire.  Either one would put her in a bad mood, and either one would generate a bit of ill will toward those that didnt help her.  Misery is contagious.  Now lets look at what actually happened.  By carrying the woman across, he left her with a good impression of the monks, brightened her day and left her with a beautiful gown.  When she got to where she was going, not only would her appearance be good, but her mood would as well.  Joy is contagious too.
             </p>
             <p>
              What the young monk saw was a block, a challenge to his faith, something to be resisted.  That was increased even further when he chose to continue to carry that long after the river was crossed.  He didnt see, or look for, the way to bring compassion and kindness to the world around him.  When the old monk scolded him, he did it in terms the young monk would understand.  The burden is gone, yet you carry it in your mind.
             </p>
             <p>
              For the old monk, there was no problem at all.  There was simply an opportunity to spread joy because thats the way he chose to see the situation.  The young monk chose to see the situation in a different, less enlightened light, and thus carried the woman much further than the old monk did.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>
          </li>

             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              Do not be attached to anything  rules, desire, righteous self-indignation.  The story is about the 3 Poisons:  Attachment, Aversion, and Ignorance.
             </p>

            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              While I believe that the interpretation about this story is to help those in need, I also think the ultimate lesson here is not to carry your past with you, rather, live in the present.  The old monk clearly states that he let the girl off at the other side of the river, while figuratively, the younger monk is still carrying her.  The moral is to not let the emotions and things that make your angry stay with you.
             </p>

             <p>
              I thought, being that this is a Buddhist story, the moral was to not hold to such fast and steady rules.  Life is fluidity and being so rigid will only create pain in your life.  The older monk broke the rule because he knew no rules are absolute.  When the younger monk is still distressed even after the event has happened, the older monk shows him that he is only creating pain for himself unnecessarily by still carrying her even though its over and done.

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="comment-41869">
           <div class="comment-avatar">
            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/8a7c333a967d3d8b1ea496c6162185f0?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/8a7c333a967d3d8b1ea496c6162185f0?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="Caveman" width="64"/>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-content">
            <div class="comment-author vcard">
             <cite>
              Caveman
             </cite>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Saturday, February 15th 2014, 12:23am">
              February 15, 2014, 12:23 am
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a href="#comment-41869" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Caveman" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=41869#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-41869", "41869", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              Zen practizes are about accepting what the present moment brings, and then at the next second let it go with no attachements to it. Thats why it focuses on breathing: accepting, then letting go, then accepting whats next, then letting go. It compares the content of the mind as waves on a beach: come and go. So the Zen person is all the time pure of any ideas inside the head, being memories about the past (either far or recent) or wishes for the future. The zen man is 100% focused on the present and gives importance only to it, since nothing else really exist.
              <br/>
              So the older monk accepts what the present offers, do what must be done, and then continues ahead with no attachments to it (no guilt, no suffer, no obsession, no self-judgement)
              <br/>
              Any attachment to an idea that is linked to the past is an excuse to not being in the present.
              <br/>
              That was the lesson.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>

            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-64 photo instant nocorner itxtalt" height="64" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/3a0f7794f04d4ff3197698a6b1e0ae79?s=64&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r" srcset="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/3a0f7794f04d4ff3197698a6b1e0ae79?s=128&amp;d=blank&amp;r=r 2x" title="Colleen" width="64"/>

            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Wednesday, April 1st 2015, 10:49am">
              April 1, 2015, 10:49 am
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">

              <a href="#comment-68636" title="Permalink to comment">

             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to Colleen" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=68636#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-68636", "68636", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              What this story means to me in the present moment:
             </p>
             <p>
              Follow the rule by its spirit, not its letter.  If we follow a rule by the letter, we may, in a direct or indirect way, negate the purpose of the rule and its original intention.  If we keep in the forefront of our minds the spirit behind each rule, we will know when the rules we follow, which may sometimes be contradictory, apply to a given situation in the present, and which rules do not.
             </p>
             <p>
              Not touching the flesh of a woman is meant to deter monks from succumbing to sexual desire; however, in this instance, that rule does not apply because the situation is not one inviting sexual interaction.  Helping a person in need, however, is the rule that applies, for that is the rule that will make the situation complete.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">

            <div class="comment-meta-data">
             <span class="comment-time" title="Saturday, July 4th 2015, 7:22pm">
              July 4, 2015, 7:22 pm
             </span>
             <span class="comment-permalink">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a href="#comment-70395" title="Permalink to comment">
               #
              </a>
             </span>
             <span class="comment-reply-link">
              <span class="separator">
               |
              </span>
              <a aria-label="Reply to clothing manufacturer" class="comment-reply-link" href="/comic/109-zen-fable-the-river-crossing/?replytocom=70395#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-70395", "70395", "respond", "1796" )'>
               Reply
              </a>
             </span>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-text">
             <p>
              Why are you people arguing about the meaning of this? Dont you understand Zen and Buddhist philosophy AT ALL? There is no universal answer to this riddle; we each must find the answer that resonates with our being. Quit arguing; no one is right or wrong. There is no universal truth, there is only the truth that we each find within the framework of our own lives. That is the beauty of these Zen sayings: there is no right or wrong answer, there is only the answer that speaks to us as an individual.
             </p>
            </div>
           </div>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>
